Web3's early promise for artists tainted by rampant stolen works and likenesses | TechCrunch
2021
https://techcrunch.com/2022/01/27/web3s-early-promise-for-artists-tainted-by-rampant-stolen-works-and-likenesses

Web3’s early promise for artists tainted by rampant stolen works and likenesses 2 years Jillian C. York didn’t want to be a non-fungible token.
A Berlin-based author and activist, York is also the director for International Freedom of Expression at the Electronic Frontier Foundation. For some reason — York doesn’t agree with her inclusion there — her name also appears on a list of so-called cypherpunks on Wikipedia.
Cypherpunks advocate for security, encryption, privacy — three things York supported but had never made her main focus.
“Of course, I can’t edit myself off that list and I don’t identify as a cypherpunk, despite the fact that I’ve advocated for cryptography,” she said. Because she respects Wikipedia’s editing rules, York was technically forced into a group she didn’t want to join.
On Christmas Eve 2021, however, York and a number of security advocates and cypherpunks on that list appeared as NFTs on the token market OpenSea. The tokens included artist renditions of each of the cypherpunks and York’s card featured her signature buzzcut peeking out from what looked like a background of circuits and fingerprints. She was now part of another group she didn’t want to join: those whose art or work had been stolen to make NFTs. She was outraged. First, the photo the creators used was copyright-protected and not actually her property.
Second, they spelled her name wrong.
Really didn’t think I’d be a fucking NFT this soon — Jillian C. York (@jilliancyork) December 26, 2021 The card, which was based on a photograph taken by a professional photographer, featured the name Jillion York. Furthermore, alongside York and her colleagues, the NFT collection featured outcasts in the security space like Richard Stallman and Jacob Appelbaum. York and several other people depicted in the cards wanted nothing to do with them.
“I don’t approve of this whatsoever and would like it removed,” tweeted York on December 26. Many other supporters and victims popped up with similar comments. A back and forth with OpenSea and the NFT creator, a company called ItsBlockchain, answered requests to remove all of the NFTs.
Many saw the irony in having to visit a central location to destroy a decentralized asset.
“Pretty absurd, and distressing, that in the new realm of Web3 digital property rights people can have their identities tokenized, without their consent, and sold as tradable commodities for the profit of others,” wrote Jacob Silverman, an editor for the New Republic.
York’s ordeal was over almost as soon as it began. The creator of the NFTs, Hitesh Malviya, contacted York and others and agreed to take down the images. In a few days, they were gone, replaced by a Medium post in which Malviya wrote that his team wanted to “educate the young community in crypto about Cypher Punks and how significant they were to this date to the evolution of blockchain technology.” “Unfortunately, many Cypher Punks were against this idea and didn’t want to participate in any way,” he wrote. “So we apologize to each and every Cypher Punk for not taking consent and creating your NFTs.” Malviya was testy when I asked him about the NFTs and why he thought he could use private photos and information — essentially someone’s art — for this money-making venture.
“We were not aware of the likeness laws in NFTs as the market is not regulated,” he said in a direct message. “And we spent three months of resources and time to create an educational series and this NFT collection. We learnt our lessons. I hope you got your answers. No more comments.” York’s situation and the resulting tumult of commentary is part of a growing and confusing part of web3: When everything is permissionless, when do you need to get permission to use someone’s face, art, or data? And, more importantly, what’s to stop bad actors from turning everything, from your t-shirt design to even your naked body, into an NFT? NFTs and the future history of art Unfortunately, York’s situation is not new, and it’s creating an entirely new industry and toolchain aimed at protecting creators from get-rich-quick NFT creators.
Another wholesale NFT heist happened in April 2021 when artist Qing Han aka Quinni’s work was stolen and reposted on the same platform that York used, OpenSea. Quinni, beloved by fans for her artistic takes on health and chronic illness, died of cancer in February 2020. After her death, her brother and fellow artist, Ze Han, maintained her social media accounts and posted her work.
A year later, thieves posted Quinni’s work anonymously. After fan outcry, the art was taken off various NFT sites, including OpenSea, and, as of this writing, all of it has been ostensibly removed from the blockchain. Her brother refuses to take part in NFTs after the theft.
“A reminder to report any of Qinni’s artwork being sold without authorization,” wrote Ze Han on Twitter. “There are no legitimate avenues where Qinni’s art is being sold (this may change in the future).” This case forced many creators to become educated in NFTs. Developers created a number of tools that help creators, many who have no interest in cryptocurrency at all, find their stolen art while Twitter feeds popped up to highlight the thefts.
OpenSea is now rejecting takedown requests (sent via their own Takedown Form) on stolen artwork, citing their ToS as the reason. We've heard from multiple artists who have sent proof of ownership, but OpenSea refused to take the work down. This thread has more info.⬇️ https://t.co/GBJBSHzmED — NFT thefts (@NFTtheft) December 17, 2021 One major figure in the online sharing community, DeviantArt, is familiar with wholesale art theft.
“We host over half a billion pieces of art on the platform,” said Liat Karpel Gurwicz, DeviantArt’s CMO. “Over the years we’ve dealt with theft and it’s nothing new. It’s something that we’ve always dealt with being an online art community even prior to there being actual regulation around it.” Most recently the company created a bot that searches for user art on the blockchain. The bot compares art on popular NFT sites like OpenSea with images by registered users. Using machine learning, the bot finds art that looks similar to art already posted on DA’s servers. It streamlines the takedown process as well, showing artists how to contact Opensea and other providers.
DeviantArt COO Moti Levy said that the system doesn’t yet discriminate between art posted by legitimate owners and hijackers.
“If we find something that is a near-identical match, we will update our users,” he said. “In some cases, it might be their NFT. We don’t know who minted it.” The company is finding success with the tool. DeviantArt Protect has already found 80,000 possible infringement cases with a 300% increase in notices sent between November and mid-December 2021. The company has also added anti-bot tools that keep NFT creators from swooping up whole collections of art as NFTs.
Ironically, the decentralized markets selling NFTs are starting to centralize around one or two providers. One of the most popular, OpenSea, has a full takedown team dedicated to situations like York’s or Quinni’s.
The company has taken off, reaching a stratospheric $13 billion valuation after a $300 million round in early January. The company is far and away the biggest player in the NFT market, with an estimated 1.26 million active users and over 80 million NFTs. According to DappRadar , the platform took in $3.27 billion in transactions in the last 30 days and managed 2.33 million transactions. Its nearest competitor, Rarible, saw $14.92 million in transactions in the same period.
NFT kingpin OpenSea lands monster $13.3B valuation in new raise OpenSea has been open about its place in the ecosystem and claims that it is managing takedown requests by artists as quickly as it can.
“​​It is against our policy to sell NFTs that violate the publicity rights of others,” said an OpenSea spokesperson. “We regularly enforce this in multiple ways, including delisting and banning accounts when we are notified that usage of a likeness is not authorized.” Interestingly, the company also seems to be cracking down on deep fakes or, as OpenSea calls it, non-consensual intimate imagery (NCII), a problem that hasn’t surfaced widely yet but could become pernicious for influencers and media stars.
“We have a zero-tolerance policy for NCII,” they said. “NFTs using NCII or similar images (including images doctored to look like someone that they are not) are prohibited, and we move quickly to ban accounts that post this material. We are actively expanding our efforts across customer support, trust and safety, and site integrity so we can move faster to protect and empower our community and creators.” OpenSea’s efforts haven’t satisfied plenty of artists, many of whom were already skeptical of NFTs before they saw their own work and colleagues’ work hijacked on their platform. Many users are still finding their art on OpenSea and, when they publicly complain, they are inundated with support scammers who purport to be official representatives of platforms like OpenSea.
Because of this mess, Levy at DeviantArt said the company is exploring NFTs but refuses to offer them yet. In fact, he thinks his users don’t want them.
“In the long term, we think that Web3 is interesting and has potential, but for us, it would have to be done in a better way and in a way that protected artists and empowered them, not in a way that puts them in danger.” As more artists and musicians turn their attention to NFTs, so, likely, do money launderers "speakable-summary\"> "font-weight: 400;\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/wework\/\">WeWork<\/a> is on the verge of filing for Chapter 11 bankruptcy in New Jersey, according to sources cited by The <\/span> "https:\/\/www.wsj.com\/articles\/wework-plans-to-file-for-bankruptcy-as-early-as-next-week-1fdcb6a5\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> "font-weight: 400;\">Wall Street Journal<\/span><\/a> "font-weight: 400;\">.<\/span><\/p>\n If WeWork does indeed file, it shouldn’t come as a shock to close followers of the f "font-weight: 400;\">lexible workspace provider. "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/19\/epic-and-matchs-antitrust-case-against-google-heads-to-jury-trial-on-november-6\/\">antitrust battle Match waged against the tech giant,<\/a> even as the court case continues with Fortnite maker Epic Games. As part of the settlement agreement, Match Group — which owns dating apps Tinder, Hinge, OKCupid, Match.com and others — will be able to implement “ "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/09\/02\/google-will-allow-alternative-payment-systems-for-play-store-in-more-countries\/\">user choice billing<\/a>” by March 31, 2024. This feature allows users to pay with other systems besides Google’s own.<\/p>\n Spotify and Bumble were among the initial testers for the new system, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/10\/google-play-to-pilot-third-party-billing-in-new-markets-including-u-s-bumble-joins-spotify-as-early-tester\/\">first introduced in November 2022<\/a>. Since then, Spotify’s third-party billing option "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/31\/spotifys-third-party-billing-option-has-now-reached-over-140-global-markets\/\"> rolled out to worldwide markets<\/a> as Google expanded user choice billing to "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/10\/google-play-to-pilot-third-party-billing-in-new-markets-including-u-s-bumble-joins-spotify-as-early-tester\/\">more countries outside the U.S.<\/a><\/p>\n The program, so far, has not allowed third-party app markers that charge users for purchases and subscriptions to do away entirely with the commission Google requires. Instead, it offers a small percentage off the standard 15% to 30% commission. Currently, that discount is 4%, "https:\/\/support.google.com\/googleplay\/android-developer\/answer\/12570971\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">per Google’s help documentation.<\/a> The program is also still a pilot open to all developers globally who are selling apps to users in the supported 35 markets where it’s now available. Google has not said when the program will become globally available.<\/p>\n As part of the agreement, Match Group will not be allowed to only<\/em> offer its own billing system — it will offer its system alongside the existing Google Play billing system. Users will be able to choose which way they want to pay.<\/p>\n In addition, the settlement agreement includes a provision that Google will continue to work with Match Group in other areas where the two companies partner, including in Google Cloud and in the use of Google’s AI technologies, a Google spokesperson said. This suggests that the rift between the two tech companies incurred by the lawsuit could have led to more complicated relationships across other areas of both Google and Match’s business.<\/p>\n Like Apple, Google has long stressed that its commission structure is not only based on its ability to process payments for its customers. Instead, it suggests that the fees help to support the entire Google Play and Android app ecosystem, including hosting, app discovery, developer tools and other resources necessary to create an app business.<\/p>\n But as larger companies like Match and Epic have argued, they don’t need the same resources as other developers. In fact, "https:\/\/www.fortnite.com\/mobile\/android\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">as Epic demonstrated<\/a>, they could self-distribute apps and manage their own payments. They believed they should then at least have the option to have a more direct relationship with their paying customers, and Google preventing this was an anti-competitive concern.<\/p>\n “We are pleased to reach a settlement agreement with Match Group,” a Google spokesperson said, in a prepared statement. “This ensures we can continue to provide our shared users the secure, seamless, and high-quality experience people expect from apps on Google Play while maintaining Google\u2019s ability to invest in the Android ecosystem and deliver value across an app\u2019s full lifecycle,” they added.<\/p>\n “Today, the Match plaintiffs and Google informed the court that they have reached a binding term sheet for a settlement of their respective claims against each other in the Match Group, LLC et al vs. Google LLC et al lawsuit. Under the terms, the $40 million placed in escrow will be returned to Match Group and no other amounts will be owed by the Match plaintiffs to Google relating to the claims in the lawsuit for the period ending December 31, 2023,” a Match Group statement added, shared alongside the release of its Q3 earnings results.<\/p>\n Recently, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/11\/google-pushes-match-for-more-money-in-antitrust-battle-court-filing-states\/\"> Google had pushed Match Group for more money in escrow<\/a>, saying that the $40 million would not be enough to cover the missed payments. (Match had been allowed to escrow funds to cover commissions while the lawsuit was underway.)<\/p>\n Match noted that the parties agreed that by March 31, 2024, its apps will implement Google\u2019s User Choice Billing, reducing its commission payments from 15% and 30% to 11% and 26%, respectively.<\/p>\n “The parties will enter a new partnership agreement that will provide value exchange across their broad relationship, which we expect will essentially offset the additional costs that Match Group brands expect to incur over the three years starting in 2024 associated with the implementation and continued use of User Choice Billing in compliance with Google\u2019s payment policy during that period,” Match Group also said.<\/p>\n In addition to the settlement news, Match Group reported a 9% year-over-year increase in revenue in Q3 to $882 million, with Tinder’s portion up 11% to $509 million.<\/p>\n It’s unclear whether this settlement will prompt Epic Games to also reach an agreement with the tech giant. If it does not, it’s set to go to trial next week to present its arguments.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"qGcXVnzmF8\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/01\/19\/epic-and-matchs-antitrust-case-against-google-heads-to-jury-trial-on-november-6\/\">Epic and Match’s antitrust case against Google heads to jury trial on November 6<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; la-wins-fatal-autopilot-crash-jury-trial\/","title":{"rendered":"Tesla wins fatal Autopilot crash jury trial"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\">A jury on Tuesday handed Tesla another win after siding with the automaker over allegations that its Autopilot advanced driver assistance system led to a death.<\/p>\n The case, which was being tried in the Superior Court of California, County of Riverside, was filed by two passengers who survived a 2019 crash and alleged that Tesla knew its product was defective. The two survivors sought $400 million in damages for the loss of the driver’s life, physical injury and mental anguish.<\/p>\n Tesla argued that the crash, which resulted in the death of the driver Micah Lee, was the result of human error \u2014 the same stance it’s taken in other Autopilot lawsuits.<\/p>\n While Tesla has won other lawsuits, including a California jury trial earlier this year that determined the automaker’s Autopilot system was not to blame for a 2019 crash. In that case, the "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/21\/tesla-wins-autopilot-crash-case-in-california\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">jury awarded no damages<\/a> to Los Angeles resident Justine Hsu, who sued Tesla in 2020 alleging negligence, fraud and breach of contract. The Molander v. Tesla case that wrapped up Tuesday was the first jury trial involving a fatality.<\/p>\n Tesla still faces a number of other lawsuits in California, including a wrongful death suit that was filed by the family of Walter Huang, an Apple engineer who died after his Tesla Model X with Autopilot engaged crashed into a highway median. The State of California Department of Transportation is also named in the lawsuit. That wrongful death lawsuit, filed in California Superior Court, County of Santa Clara, alleges that errors by Tesla\u2019s Autopilot driver assistance system caused the crash that killed Huang on March 23, 2018. Huang, who was 38, died when his 2017 Tesla Model X hit a highway barrier on Highway 101 in Mountain View, California. A jury trial is expected to begin next year in that case.<\/p>\n Tesla is also facing scrutiny from "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/29\/nhtsa-raises-more-concerns-about-teslas-autopilot-safety\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">federal and state regulators,<\/a> all of which are related to Autopilot and its upgraded version known as Full Self-Driving.<\/p>\n Tesla vehicles come standard with a driver-assistance system branded as Autopilot. For a $6,000 upgrade, owners can purchase Enhanced Autopilot, which includes several other features, including an active guidance system that navigates a car from a highway on-ramp to off-ramp, including interchanges and making lane changes.<\/p> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n For an\u00a0additional $12,000, owners can buy \u201cfull self-driving,\u201d or FSD \u2014 a feature that CEO\u00a0 "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/elon-musk\/\" data-mrf-link=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/elon-musk\/\">Elon Musk<\/a>\u00a0has promised for years will one day deliver full autonomous driving capabilities.<\/p>\n Tesla vehicles\u00a0 "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/07\/tesla-refutes-elon-musks-timeline-on-full-self-driving\/\" data-mrf-link=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/05\/07\/tesla-refutes-elon-musks-timeline-on-full-self-driving\/\">are not self-driving<\/a>. Instead, FSD includes a number of automated driving features that still require the driver to be ready to take control at all times. It includes everything in Enhanced Autopilot and is supposed to handle steering on city streets and recognize and react to traffic lights and stop signs.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":" A jury on Tuesday handed Tesla another win after siding with the automaker over allegations that its Autopilot advanced driver assistance system led to a death. The case, which was being tried in the Superior Court of California, County of Riverside, was filed by two passengers who survived a 2019 crash and alleged that Tesla ","title":{"rendered":"So long, Twitter Circles"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\">If tic-tac-toe has taught us anything, it’s that Xs and circles cannot peacefully coexist.<\/p>\n X, the app formerly known as Twitter, has shut down the Circles feature, which allowed users to post to a small, exclusive audience. Like an Instagram Close Friends story, Circles — n\u00e9e Twitter Circles — offered a more intimate social media experience. In other words, you could overshare about your life to an audience of friends only, rather than the whole internet. And isn’t one of the greatest joys of the internet to emotionally shitpost without worrying about the consequences?<\/p>\n But, as the whims of the Circle reminded us, nothing we say on the internet is ever actually free from consequence. The feature worked almost all of the time. Almost. And anything less than 100% efficacy is not ideal for a feature that people used to share private thoughts with trusted friends.<\/p>\n As early as February, some users reported that their Circle tweets were "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/02\/03\/twitter-circle-glitches-have-users-worried-about-privacy\/\">reaching a wider audience<\/a> than they were supposed to. These glitches showed up "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/10\/twitter-circle-bug-not-private\/\">again in April<\/a>, and X (then known as Twitter) "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/05\/05\/twitter-confirms-circle-tweets-temporarily-were-not-private\/\">apologized<\/a> for the error in May, marking a rare moment of transparency for the company.<\/p>\n Even when Circle tweets were truly private, they often appeared not to be — the green banner denoting it as a Circle post only appeared some of the time (I had received some paranoid texts from friends who wondered if I knew I had posted about [redacted] on main, but nope, this was just the glitchy old bird app playing tricks on us).<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--twitter\">\n "twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n "en\" dir=\"ltr\">I can't believe this website is going to take away my most treasured joke: my Twitter circle that only contains Tony Hawk.
"https:\/\/t.co\/P9pRF2HTIy\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">pic.twitter.com\/P9pRF2HTIy<\/a><\/p>\n — Giovanni Colantonio (@MarioPrime) "https:\/\/twitter.com\/MarioPrime\/status\/1719398305526861961?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">October 31, 2023<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n "https:\/\/platform.twitter.com\/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"><\/script><\/div>\n But perhaps nothing is sadder than the loss of "https:\/\/twitter.com\/MarioPrime\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">one user<\/a>‘s “most treasured joke,” in which he posted to a Circle audience consisting only of Tony Hawk, because you could add people who didn’t follow you to your Circle. And they could not remove themselves.<\/p>\n Some users have expressed concern that their private musings would be made public, now that Circles are no longer. So far, this doesn’t seem to be the case, though you’re able to view your own Circle tweets on your profile. But on X, who knows when something will break, so if you have anything salacious up, it might be worth deleting.<\/p>\n It’s not clear why "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/09\/21\/x-is-shutting-down-its-circle-feature-in-october\/\">X is sunsetting Circles<\/a> — perhaps it will come back as a paid benefit, like another beloved feature, "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/08\/15\/x-formerly-twitter-makes-x-pro-formerly-tweetdeck-a-subscriber-only-product\/\">TweetDeck<\/a> (now called X Pro). But users will understandably be annoyed if they have to pay money to X to regain features they used to have for free.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"i0QNe6bERD\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/04\/10\/twitter-circle-bug-not-private\/\">Twitter Circle tweets are not that private anymore<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n ndered":"SBF\u2019s defense puts forth a 35-minute last-ditch effort to show his goodwill"},"content":{"rendered":" "speakable-summary\"> "featured__span-first-words\">As Sam Bankman-Fried<\/span> took the stand for the last time in his trial for fraud and money laundering, his main lawyer, Mark Cohen, returned to question him on a number of themes that prosecutors touched on in the past few days. But this time, he took a different approach, presenting the defendant as a person who was acting in good faith.<\/p>\n \u201cWe lived and died by having a better product than competitors,\u201d Bankman-Fried said of his "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/11\/11\/ftx-files-for-bankruptcy-ceo-sam-bankman-fried-steps-down\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">now-bankrupt crypto exchange<\/a>, FTX. Bankman-Fried owned majority stakes in both FTX and its sister trading firm, Alameda Research, but insisted he wanted the best for both.<\/p>\n Bankman-Fried has been "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/10\/26\/sam-bankman-fried-takes-the-stand\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">testifying since Thursday afternoon<\/a>, and the prosecution has grilled him for the past few days as "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2023\/10\/30\/sam-bankman-fried-ftx-cross-examination\/\">part of their cross-examination<\/a>. On Tuesday, when the defense lawyers returned to reexamine him, he was swaying slightly on the stand, shoulders drawn down and forward. He spoke softly, but was more talkative than earlier in the day and on Monday when he was being questioned.<\/p>\n Contrary to the picture prosecutors tried to paint, Bankman-Fried insisted that he was not involved in the day-to-day trading operations of Alameda or its \u201ccore operations,\u201d but instead emphasized his concern for its leadership at the time, saying he wanted to be involved in many of its venture investments, decisions on hedging, and other areas.

York didn\u2019t want to be a non-fungible token.<\/p>\n A Berlin-based author and activist, York is also the director for International Freedom of Expression at the Electronic Frontier Foundation. For some reason — York doesn\u2019t agree with her inclusion there — her name also appears on a list of so-called "https:\/\/en.wikipedia.org\/wiki\/Cypherpunk\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">cypherpunks on Wikipedia.<\/a> Cypherpunks advocate for security, encryption, privacy — three things York supported but had never made her main focus.<\/p>\n \u201cOf course, I can’t edit myself off that list and I don’t identify as a cypherpunk, despite the fact that I’ve advocated for cryptography,\u201d she said. Because she respects Wikipedia\u2019s editing rules, York was technically forced into a group she didn\u2019t want to join.<\/p>\n On Christmas Eve 2021, however, York and a number of security advocates and cypherpunks on that list appeared as NFTs on the token market OpenSea. The tokens included artist renditions of each of the cypherpunks and York\u2019s card featured her signature buzzcut peeking out from what looked like a background of circuits and fingerprints. She was now part of another group she didn\u2019t want to join: those whose art or work had been stolen to make NFTs. She was outraged. First, the photo the creators used was copyright-protected and not actually her property.<\/p>\n Second, they spelled her name wrong.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--twitter\">\n "twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n "en\" dir=\"ltr\">Really didn\u2019t think I\u2019d be a fucking NFT this soon<\/p>\n — Jillian C. York (@jilliancyork) "https:\/\/twitter.com\/jilliancyork\/status\/1475165335154302985?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">December 26, 2021<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote> "piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n "https:\/\/platform.twitter.com\/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"><\/script><\/div>\n The card, which was based on a photograph taken by a professional photographer, featured the name Jillion York. Furthermore, alongside York and her colleagues, the NFT collection featured outcasts in the security space like Richard Stallman and Jacob Appelbaum. York and several other people depicted in the cards wanted nothing to do with them.<\/p>\n \u201cI don\u2019t approve of this whatsoever and would like it removed,\u201d "https:\/\/mobile.twitter.com\/jilliancyork\/status\/1475164895020781579\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">tweeted<\/a> York on December 26. Many other supporters and victims popped up with similar comments. A back and forth with OpenSea and the NFT creator, a company called ItsBlockchain, "https:\/\/medium.com\/@cipherpunks\/cipher-punks-nft-collection-shuts-down-gives-back-all-the-funds-8e6d00299d68\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">answered<\/a> requests to remove all of the NFTs.<\/p>\n Many saw the irony in having to visit a central location to destroy a decentralized asset.<\/p>\n \u201cPretty absurd, and distressing, that in the new realm of Web3 digital property rights people can have their identities tokenized, without their consent, and sold as tradable commodities for the profit of others,\u201d "https:\/\/mobile.twitter.com\/SilvermanJacob\/status\/1475166498033332226\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">wrote<\/a> Jacob Silverman, an editor for the New Republic.<\/p>\n York\u2019s ordeal was over almost as soon as it began. The creator of the NFTs, Hitesh Malviya, contacted York and others and agreed to take down the images. In a few days, they were gone, replaced by a Medium post in which Malviya "https:\/\/medium.com\/@cipherpunks\/cipher-punks-nft-collection-shuts-down-gives-back-all-the-funds-8e6d00299d68\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">wrote<\/a> that his team wanted to \u201ceducate the young community in crypto about Cypher Punks and how significant they were to this date to the evolution of blockchain technology.\u201d<\/p>\n \u201cUnfortunately, many Cypher Punks were against this idea and didn\u2019t want to participate in any way,\u201d he wrote. \u201cSo we apologize to each and every Cypher Punk for not taking consent and creating your NFTs.\u201d<\/p>\n Malviya was testy when I asked him about the NFTs and why he thought he could use private photos and information — essentially someone\u2019s art — for this money-making venture.<\/p>\n \u201cWe were not aware of the likeness laws in NFTs as the market is not regulated,\u201d he said in a direct message. \u201cAnd we spent three months of resources and time to create an educational series and this NFT collection. We learnt our lessons. I hope you got your answers. No more comments.”<\/p>\n York\u2019s situation and the resulting tumult of commentary is part of a growing and confusing part of web3: When everything is permissionless, when do you need to get permission to use someone\u2019s face, art, or data? And, more importantly, what\u2019s to stop bad actors from turning everything, from your t-shirt design to even your naked body, into an NFT?<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"CMSKJu6WG4\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/11\/01\/nfts-and-the-future-history-of-art\/\">NFTs and the future history of art<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“NFTs and the future history of art” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/11\/01\/nfts-and-the-future-history-of-art\/embed\/#?secret=gjHijtkzY1#?secret=CMSKJu6WG4\" data-secret=\"CMSKJu6WG4\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n Unfortunately, York\u2019s situation is not new, and it\u2019s creating an entirely new industry and toolchain aimed at protecting creators from get-rich-quick NFT creators.<\/p>\n Another wholesale NFT heist happened in April 2021 when artist Qing Han aka Quinni\u2019s work was stolen and reposted on the same platform that York used, OpenSea. Quinni, beloved by fans for her artistic takes on health and chronic illness, died of cancer in February 2020. After her death, her brother and fellow artist, Ze Han, maintained her social media accounts and posted her work.<\/p>\n A year later, thieves posted Quinni\u2019s work anonymously. After fan outcry, the art was taken off various NFT sites, including OpenSea, and, as of this writing, all of it has been ostensibly removed from the blockchain. Her brother refuses to take part in NFTs after the theft.<\/p>\n \u201cA reminder to report any of Qinni’s artwork being sold without authorization,\u201d wrote Ze Han on Twitter. \u201cThere are no legitimate avenues where Qinni’s art is being sold (this may change in the future).\u201d<\/p>\n This case forced many creators to become educated in NFTs. Developers created a number of tools that help creators, many who have no interest in cryptocurrency at all, find their stolen art while "https:\/\/twitter.com\/NFTtheft\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Twitter feeds popped up<\/a> to highlight the thefts.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--twitter\">\n "twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n "en\" dir=\"ltr\">OpenSea is now rejecting takedown requests (sent via their own Takedown Form) on stolen artwork, citing their ToS as the reason. We've heard from multiple artists who have sent proof of ownership, but OpenSea refused to take the work down. This thread has more info.\u2b07\ufe0f "https:\/\/t.co\/GBJBSHzmED\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">https:\/\/t.co\/GBJBSHzmED<\/a><\/p>\n — NFT thefts (@NFTtheft) "https:\/\/twitter.com\/NFTtheft\/status\/1471640210588852227?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">December 17, 2021<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "https:\/\/platform.twitter.com\/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"><\/script><\/div>\n One major figure in the online sharing community, DeviantArt, is familiar with wholesale art theft.<\/p>\n \u201cWe host over half a billion pieces of art on the platform,\u201d said Liat Karpel Gurwicz, DeviantArt\u2019s CMO. \u201cOver the years we\u2019ve dealt with theft and it\u2019s nothing new. It’s something that we’ve always dealt with being an online art community even prior to there being actual regulation around it.\u201d<\/p>\n Most recently the company created a bot that searches for user art on the blockchain. The bot compares art on popular NFT sites like OpenSea with images by registered users. Using machine learning, the bot finds art that looks similar to art already posted on DA\u2019s servers. It streamlines the takedown process as well, showing artists how to contact Opensea and other providers.<\/p>\n DeviantArt COO Moti Levy said that the system doesn\u2019t yet discriminate between art posted by legitimate owners and hijackers.<\/p>\n \u201cIf we find something that is a near-identical match, we will update our users,\u201d he said. \u201cIn some cases, it might be their NFT. We don\u2019t know who minted it.\u201d<\/p>\n The company is finding success with the tool. DeviantArt Protect has already found 80,000 possible infringement cases with a 300% increase in notices sent between November and mid-December 2021. The company has also added anti-bot tools that keep NFT creators from swooping up whole collections of art as NFTs.<\/p>\n Ironically, the decentralized markets selling NFTs are starting to centralize around one or two providers. One of the most popular, OpenSea, has a full takedown team dedicated to situations like York\u2019s or Quinni\u2019s.<\/p>\n The company has taken off, reaching a stratospheric $13 billion valuation after a $300 million round in early January. The company is far and away the biggest player in the NFT market, with an estimated 1.26 million active users and over 80 million NFTs. According to "https:\/\/dappradar.com\/ethereum\/marketplaces\/opensea\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">DappRadar<\/a>, the platform took in $3.27 billion in transactions in the last 30 days and managed 2.33 million transactions. Its nearest competitor, Rarible, saw $14.92 million in transactions in the same period.<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"Ltt1p78eud\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/04\/nft-kingpin-opensea-lands-13-3b-valuation-in-300m-raise-from-paradigm-and-coatue\/\">NFT kingpin OpenSea lands monster $13.3B valuation in new raise<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“NFT kingpin OpenSea lands monster $13.3B valuation in new raise” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/01\/04\/nft-kingpin-opensea-lands-13-3b-valuation-in-300m-raise-from-paradigm-and-coatue\/embed\/#?secret=Z5eBWMWUUi#?secret=Ltt1p78eud\" data-secret=\"Ltt1p78eud\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n OpenSea has been open about its place in the ecosystem and claims that it is managing takedown requests by artists as quickly as it can.<\/p>\n \u201c\u200b\u200bIt is against our policy to sell NFTs that violate the publicity rights of others,\u201d said an OpenSea spokesperson. \u201cWe regularly enforce this in multiple ways, including delisting and banning accounts when we are notified that usage of a likeness is not authorized.\u201d<\/p>\n Interestingly, the company also seems to be cracking down on deep fakes or, as OpenSea calls it, non-consensual intimate imagery (NCII), a problem that hasn\u2019t surfaced widely yet but could become pernicious for influencers and media stars.<\/p>\n \u201cWe have a zero-tolerance policy for NCII,\u201d they said. \u201cNFTs using NCII or similar images (including images doctored to look like someone that they are not) are prohibited, and we move quickly to ban accounts that post this material. We are actively expanding our efforts across customer support, trust and safety, and site integrity so we can move faster to protect and empower our community and creators.”<\/p>\n OpenSea’s efforts haven’t satisfied plenty of artists, many of whom were already skeptical of NFTs before they saw their own work and colleagues\u2019 work hijacked on their platform. Many users are still finding their art on OpenSea and, when they publicly complain, they are "https:\/\/twitter.com\/BlindHag\/status\/1480317000815632395\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> inundated with support scammers<\/a> who purport to be official representatives of platforms like OpenSea.<\/p>\n Because of this mess, Levy at DeviantArt said the company is exploring NFTs but refuses to offer them yet. In fact, he thinks his users don\u2019t want them.<\/p>\n \u201cIn the long term, we think that Web3 is interesting and has potential, but for us, it would have to be done in a better way and in a way that protected artists and empowered them, not in a way that puts them in danger.\u201d<\/p>\n "embed breakout embed-oembed embed--techcrunch\">\n "wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"dFTPC7tkPU\"> "https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/03\/24\/nft_users\/\">As more artists and musicians turn their attention to NFTs, so, likely, do money launderers<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n "wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"“As more artists and musicians turn their attention to NFTs, so, likely, do money launderers” — TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/03\/24\/nft_users\/embed\/#?secret=y1IGNlCiPK#?secret=dFTPC7tkPU\" data-secret=\"dFTPC7tkPU\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":" Jillian C. For some reason — York doesn\u2019t agree with her inclusion there — her name also appears on a list of so-called cypherpunks on Wikipedia.
